text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>O)</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Ices</b>:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> (NH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>3</span>)</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)" title="Water (molecule)">water</a> (H<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>O)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a> (NH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>SH)</li></ul>\n</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Jupiter</b> is the fifth <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size" title="List of Solar System objects by size">largest</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planet</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> one-thousandth that of the Sun, but two-and-a-half times that of all the other planets in the Solar System combined. Jupiter and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> are <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>; the other two giant planets, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>. Jupiter has been known to <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomers</a> since antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup> It is named after the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman god</a> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> When viewed from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, Jupiter can reach an <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of &#8722;2.94, bright enough for its <a href="/wiki/Planetshine" title="Planetshine">reflected light</a> to cast shadows,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> and making it on average the third-brightest natural object in the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>.\n</p><p>Jupiter is primarily composed of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> with a quarter of its mass being <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, though helium comprises only about a tenth of the number of molecules. It may also have a rocky core of heavier elements,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coreuncertainty_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-coreuncertainty-23">[20]</a></sup> but like the other giant planets, Jupiter lacks a well-defined solid surface. Because of its rapid rotation, the planet\'s shape is that of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroid</a> (it has a slight but noticeable bulge around the equator). The outer atmosphere is visibly segregated into several bands at different latitudes, resulting in turbulence and storms along their interacting boundaries. A prominent result is the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a>, a giant storm that is known to have existed since at least the 17th century when it was first seen by <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>. Surrounding Jupiter is a faint <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">planetary ring</a> system and a powerful <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>. Jupiter has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">79 known moons</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> including the four large <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> discovered by <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> in 1610. <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, the largest of these, has a diameter greater than that of the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>.\n</p><p>Jupiter has been explored on several occasions by <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic spacecraft</a>, most notably during the early <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_program" title="Pioneer program">Pioneer</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flyby</a> missions and later by the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)"><i>Galileo</i> orbiter</a>. In late February 2007, Jupiter was visited by the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> probe, which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">used Jupiter\'s gravity</a> to increase its speed and bend its trajectory en route to <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>. The latest probe to visit the planet is <i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i>, which entered into orbit around Jupiter on July 4, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160705_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160705-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160630_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160630-26">[23]</a></sup> Future targets for exploration in the Jupiter system include the probable ice-covered liquid ocean of its moon <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_migration">Formation and migration</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grand_tack_hypothesis" title="Grand tack hypothesis">Grand tack hypothesis</a></div> <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Formation and evolution of the Solar System</a></div>\n<p>Astronomers have discovered nearly 500 planetary systems with multiple planets. Regularly these systems include a few planets with masses several times greater than Earth\'s (<a href="/wiki/Super-Earth" title="Super-Earth">super-Earths</a>), orbiting closer to their star than Mercury is to the Sun, and sometimes also Jupiter-mass gas giants close to their star.\n</p><p>Earth and its neighbor planets may have formed from fragments of planets after collisions with Jupiter destroyed those super-Earths near the Sun. As Jupiter came toward the inner Solar System, in what theorists call the <a href="/wiki/Grand_tack_hypothesis" title="Grand tack hypothesis">grand tack hypothesis</a>, gravitational tugs and pulls occurred causing a series of collisions between the super-Earths as their orbits began to overlap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jupiter moving out of the inner Solar System would have allowed the formation of inner planets, including <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>\n<p>Jupiter is composed primarily of gaseous and liquid matter. It is the largest of the four giant planets in the Solar System and hence its largest planet. It has a diameter of 142,984&#160;km (88,846&#160;mi) at its <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. The average density of Jupiter, 1.326&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, is the second highest of the giant planets, but lower than those of the four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h3>\n<p>Jupiter\'s upper atmosphere is about 88&#8211;92% hydrogen and 8&#8211;12% helium by percent volume of gas <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>. A helium atom has about four times as much mass as a hydrogen atom, so the composition changes when described as the proportion of mass contributed by different atoms. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">Jupiter\'s atmosphere</a> is approximately 75% hydrogen and 24% helium by mass, with the remaining one percent of the mass consisting of other elements. The atmosphere contains trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>-based compounds. There are also traces of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>. The outermost layer of the atmosphere contains <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a> of frozen ammonia. The interior contains denser materials&#8212;by mass it is roughly 71% hydrogen, 24% helium, and 5% other elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voyager_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-voyager-29">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cassini_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-cassini-30">[27]</a></sup> Through <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> measurements, trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> have also been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The atmospheric proportions of hydrogen and helium are close to the theoretical composition of the primordial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a>. Neon in the upper atmosphere only consists of 20 parts per million by mass, which is about a tenth as abundant as in the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup> Helium is also depleted to about 80% of the Sun\'s helium composition. This depletion is a result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> of these elements into the interior of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galileo_ms_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-galileo_ms-33">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Based on <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> is thought to be similar in composition to Jupiter, but the other giant planets <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> have relatively less hydrogen and helium and relatively more <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">ices</a> and are thus now termed <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_and_size">Mass and size</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass">Jupiter mass</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SolarSystem_OrdersOfMagnitude_Sun-Jupiter-Earth-Moon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4010" data-file-width="4010" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/SolarSystem_OrdersOfMagnitude_Sun-Jupiter-Earth-Moon.jpg/220px-SolarSystem_OrdersOfMagnitude_Sun-Jupiter-Earth-Moon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/SolarSystem_OrdersOfMagnitude_Sun-Jupiter-Earth-Moon.jpg/330px-SolarSystem_OrdersOfMagnitude_Sun-Jupiter-Earth-Moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/SolarSystem_OrdersOfMagnitude_Sun-Jupiter-Earth-Moon.jpg/440px-SolarSystem_OrdersOfMagnitude_Sun-Jupiter-Earth-Moon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SolarSystem_OrdersOfMagnitude_Sun-Jupiter-Earth-Moon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter\'s diameter is one <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">order of magnitude</a> smaller (&#215;0.10045) than that of the Sun, and one order of magnitude larger (&#215;10.9733) than that of Earth. The Great Red Spot is roughly the same size as Earth.</div></div></div>\n<p>Jupiter\'s mass is 2.5 times that of all the other planets in the Solar System combined&#8212;this is so massive that its <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> lies above the <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">Sun\'s surface</a> at 1.068&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_radius" title="Solar radius">solar radii</a> from the Sun\'s center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> Jupiter is much larger than Earth and considerably less dense: its volume is that of about 1,321 Earths, but it is only 318 times as massive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-fact-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-36">[33]</a></sup> Jupiter\'s radius is about 1/10 the <a href="/wiki/Solar_radius" title="Solar radius">radius of the Sun</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shu82_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-shu82-37">[34]</a></sup> and its mass is 0.001 times the <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">mass of the Sun</a>, so the densities of the two bodies are similar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_turekian05_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis_turekian05-38">[35]</a></sup> A "<a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass">Jupiter mass</a>" (<var>M</var><sub>J</sub> or <var>M</var><sub>Jup</sub>) is often used as a unit to describe masses of other objects, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_dwarfs" title="Brown dwarfs">brown dwarfs</a>. So, for example, the extrasolar planet <a href="/wiki/HD_209458_b" title="HD 209458 b">HD 209458 b</a> has a mass of <span class="nowrap">0.69</span>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>J</sub>, while <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Andromedae_b" title="Kappa Andromedae b">Kappa Andromedae b</a> has a mass of <span class="nowrap">12.8</span>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>J</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Theoretical models indicate that if Jupiter had much more mass than it does at present, it would shrink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seager2007_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seager2007-40">[37]</a></sup> For small changes in mass, the <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> would not change appreciably, and above about 500&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#8853;</sub></a> (1.6 Jupiter masses)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seager2007_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seager2007-40">[37]</a></sup> the interior would become so much more compressed under the increased pressure that its volume would <i>decrease</i> despite the increasing amount of matter. As a result, Jupiter is thought to have about as large a diameter as a planet of its composition and evolutionary history can achieve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-41">[38]</a></sup> The process of further shrinkage with increasing mass would continue until appreciable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_ignition" title="Stellar ignition">stellar ignition</a> was achieved, as in high-mass <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a> having around 50 Jupiter masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tristan286_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-tristan286-42">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although Jupiter would need to be about 75 times as massive to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fusion" title="Hydrogen fusion">fuse hydrogen</a> and become a <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a>, the smallest <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a> is only about 30 percent larger in radius than Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup> Despite this, Jupiter still radiates more heat than it receives from the Sun; the amount of heat produced inside it is similar to the total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> it receives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup> This additional heat is generated by the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_mechanism" title="Kelvin&#8211;Helmholtz mechanism">Kelvin&#8211;Helmholtz mechanism</a> through contraction. This process causes Jupiter to shrink by about 2&#160;cm each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot04_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-guillot04-46">[43]</a></sup> When it was first formed, Jupiter was much hotter and was about twice its current diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>\n<p>Jupiter is thought to consist of a dense <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a> with a mixture of elements, a surrounding layer of liquid <a href="/wiki/Metallic_hydrogen" title="Metallic hydrogen">metallic hydrogen</a> with some helium, and an outer layer predominantly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen">molecular hydrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot04_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-guillot04-46">[43]</a></sup> Beyond this basic outline, there is still considerable uncertainty. The core is often described as <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocky</a>, but its detailed composition is unknown, as are the properties of materials at the temperatures and pressures of those depths (see below). In 1997, the existence of the core was suggested by gravitational measurements,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot04_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-guillot04-46">[43]</a></sup> indicating a mass of from 12 to 45 times that of Earth, or roughly 4%&#8211;14% of the total mass of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> The presence of a core during at least part of Jupiter\'s history is suggested by models of planetary formation that require the formation of a rocky or icy core massive enough to collect its bulk of hydrogen and helium from the <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis" title="Nebular hypothesis">protosolar nebula</a>. Assuming it did exist, it may have shrunk as convection currents of hot liquid metallic hydrogen mixed with the molten core and carried its contents to higher levels in the planetary interior. A core may now be entirely absent, as gravitational measurements are not yet precise enough to rule that possibility out entirely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot04_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-guillot04-46">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19640-Jupiter-Infrared-Animation-20150516.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="752" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/PIA19640-Jupiter-Infrared-Animation-20150516.gif/170px-PIA19640-Jupiter-Infrared-Animation-20150516.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/PIA19640-Jupiter-Infrared-Animation-20150516.gif/255px-PIA19640-Jupiter-Infrared-Animation-20150516.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/PIA19640-Jupiter-Infrared-Animation-20150516.gif/340px-PIA19640-Jupiter-Infrared-Animation-20150516.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19640-Jupiter-Infrared-Animation-20150516.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of four images showing Jupiter in infrared light as seen by <a href="/wiki/NASA_Infrared_Telescope_Facility" title="NASA Infrared Telescope Facility">NASA\'s Infrared telescope facility</a> on May 16, 2015</div></div></div>\n<p>The uncertainty of the models is tied to the error margin in hitherto measured parameters: one of the rotational coefficients (J<sub>6</sub>) used to describe the planet\'s gravitational moment, Jupiter\'s equatorial radius, and its temperature at 1 bar pressure. The <a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)"><i>Juno</i> mission</a>, which arrived in July 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160705_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160705-25">[22]</a></sup> is expected to further constrain the values of these parameters for better models of the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The core region may be surrounded by dense <a href="/wiki/Metallic_hydrogen" title="Metallic hydrogen">metallic hydrogen</a>, which extends outward to about 78% of the radius of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup> Rain-like droplets of helium and neon precipitate downward through this layer, depleting the abundance of these elements in the upper atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galileo_ms_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-galileo_ms-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">Rainfalls</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamonds</a> have been suggested to occur on Jupiter, as well as on <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SC-20131009_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-SC-20131009-52">[49]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a> <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-20170825_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-20170825-53">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Above the layer of metallic hydrogen lies a transparent interior atmosphere of hydrogen. At this depth, the pressure and temperature are above hydrogen\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_pressure" title="Critical pressure">critical pressure</a> of 1.2858 MPa and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_temperature" title="Critical temperature">critical temperature</a> of only 32.938&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> In this state, there are no distinct liquid and gas phases&#8212;hydrogen is said to be in a supercritical fluid state. It is convenient to treat hydrogen as gas in the upper layer extending downward from the cloud layer to a depth of about 1,000&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Km" title="Km">km</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup> and as liquid in deeper layers. Physically, there is no clear boundary&#8212;the gas smoothly becomes hotter and denser as one descends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang03_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang03-56">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The temperature and pressure inside Jupiter increase steadily toward the core, due to the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_mechanism" title="Kelvin&#8211;Helmholtz mechanism">Kelvin&#8211;Helmholtz mechanism</a>. At the pressure level of 10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bars</a> (1 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a>), the temperature is around 340&#160;K (67&#160;&#176;C; 152&#160;&#176;F). At the <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transition</a> region where hydrogen&#8212;heated beyond its critical point&#8212;becomes metallic, it is calculated the temperature is 10,000&#160;K (9,700&#160;&#176;C; 17,500&#160;&#176;F) and the pressure is <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(pressure)#1GPa" title="Orders of magnitude (pressure)">200</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">GPa</a>. The temperature at the core boundary is estimated to be 36,000&#160;K (35,700&#160;&#176;C; 64,300&#160;&#176;F) and the interior pressure is roughly <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(pressure)#1TPa" title="Orders of magnitude (pressure)">3,000</a>&#8211;4,500&#160;GPa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:722px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_diagram.svg"><img alt="Diagram of Jupiter\'s moons, surface, and interior" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Jupiter_diagram.svg/720px-Jupiter_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Jupiter_diagram.svg/1080px-Jupiter_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Jupiter_diagram.svg/1440px-Jupiter_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="720"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This cut-away illustrates a model of the interior of Jupiter, with a rocky core overlaid by a deep layer of liquid <a href="/wiki/Metallic_hydrogen" title="Metallic hydrogen">metallic hydrogen</a>.</div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">Atmosphere of Jupiter</a></div>\n<p>Jupiter has the largest planetary atmosphere in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, spanning over 5,000&#160;km (3,000&#160;mi) in altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sieff_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sieff-57">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_Aylward_Millward_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_Aylward_Millward-58">[55]</a></sup> Because Jupiter has no surface, the base of its atmosphere is usually considered to be the point at which atmospheric pressure is equal to 100&#160;kPa (1.0&#160;bar).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud_layers">Cloud layers</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA02863_-_Jupiter_surface_motion_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1799" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PIA02863_-_Jupiter_surface_motion_animation.gif/150px-PIA02863_-_Jupiter_surface_motion_animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PIA02863_-_Jupiter_surface_motion_animation.gif/225px-PIA02863_-_Jupiter_surface_motion_animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PIA02863_-_Jupiter_surface_motion_animation.gif/300px-PIA02863_-_Jupiter_surface_motion_animation.gif 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA02863_-_Jupiter_surface_motion_animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The movement of Jupiter\'s counter-rotating cloud bands. This looping animation maps the planet\'s exterior onto a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylindrical_projection" title="Cylindrical projection">cylindrical projection</a>.</div></div></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:176px;max-width:176px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:176px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Jupiter.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2266" data-file-width="2231" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Map_of_Jupiter.jpg/174px-Map_of_Jupiter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Map_of_Jupiter.jpg/261px-Map_of_Jupiter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Map_of_Jupiter.jpg/348px-Map_of_Jupiter.jpg 2x" width="174"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">South polar view of Jupiter</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:176px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21641-Jupiter-SouthernStorms-JunoCam-20170525.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2915" data-file-width="2646" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/PIA21641-Jupiter-SouthernStorms-JunoCam-20170525.jpg/160px-PIA21641-Jupiter-SouthernStorms-JunoCam-20170525.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/PIA21641-Jupiter-SouthernStorms-JunoCam-20170525.jpg/240px-PIA21641-Jupiter-SouthernStorms-JunoCam-20170525.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/PIA21641-Jupiter-SouthernStorms-JunoCam-20170525.jpg/320px-PIA21641-Jupiter-SouthernStorms-JunoCam-20170525.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Enhanced color view of Jupiter\'s southern storms</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Jupiter is perpetually covered with clouds composed of ammonia crystals and possibly <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a>. The clouds are located in the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a> and are arranged into bands of different latitudes, known as tropical regions. These are sub-divided into lighter-hued <i>zones</i> and darker <i>belts</i>. The interactions of these conflicting <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">circulation</a> patterns cause storms and <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wind_speed" title="Wind speed">Wind speeds</a> of 100&#160;m/s (360&#160;km/h) are common in zonal jets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> The zones have been observed to vary in width, color and intensity from year to year, but they have remained sufficiently stable for scientists to give them identifying designations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-36">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21973-AboveTheCloudsOfJupiter-JunoSpacecraft-20171216.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/PIA21973-AboveTheCloudsOfJupiter-JunoSpacecraft-20171216.jpg/150px-PIA21973-AboveTheCloudsOfJupiter-JunoSpacecraft-20171216.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/PIA21973-AboveTheCloudsOfJupiter-JunoSpacecraft-20171216.jpg/225px-PIA21973-AboveTheCloudsOfJupiter-JunoSpacecraft-20171216.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/PIA21973-AboveTheCloudsOfJupiter-JunoSpacecraft-20171216.jpg/300px-PIA21973-AboveTheCloudsOfJupiter-JunoSpacecraft-20171216.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA21973-AboveTheCloudsOfJupiter-JunoSpacecraft-20171216.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter clouds<br/>(<a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a>; December 2017)</div></div></div>\n<p>The cloud layer is only about 50&#160;km (31&#160;mi) deep, and consists of at least two decks of clouds: a thick lower deck and a thin clearer region. There may also be a thin layer of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_(properties)" title="Water (properties)">water</a> clouds underlying the ammonia layer. Supporting the idea of water clouds are the flashes of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> detected in the atmosphere of Jupiter. These electrical discharges can be up to a thousand times as powerful as lightning on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> The water clouds are assumed to generate thunderstorms in the same way as terrestrial thunderstorms, driven by the heat rising from the interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The orange and brown coloration in the clouds of Jupiter are caused by upwelling compounds that change color when they are exposed to <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light from the Sun. The exact makeup remains uncertain, but the substances are thought to be phosphorus, sulfur or possibly <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-5"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> These colorful compounds, known as <a href="/wiki/Chromophore" title="Chromophore">chromophores</a>, mix with the warmer, lower deck of clouds. The zones are formed when rising <a href="/wiki/Convection_cell" title="Convection cell">convection cells</a> form crystallizing ammonia that masks out these lower clouds from view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbook_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldbook-63">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jupiter\'s low <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> means that the poles constantly receive less <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> than at the planet\'s equatorial region. <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">Convection</a> within the interior of the planet transports more energy to the poles, balancing out the temperatures at the cloud layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-36">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Red_Spot_and_other_vortices">Great Red Spot and other vortices</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:790106-0203_Voyager_58M_to_31M_reduced.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="390" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/790106-0203_Voyager_58M_to_31M_reduced.gif/220px-790106-0203_Voyager_58M_to_31M_reduced.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/790106-0203_Voyager_58M_to_31M_reduced.gif/330px-790106-0203_Voyager_58M_to_31M_reduced.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a3/790106-0203_Voyager_58M_to_31M_reduced.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:790106-0203_Voyager_58M_to_31M_reduced.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Time-lapse sequence from the approach of <i>Voyager 1</i>, showing the motion of atmospheric bands and circulation of the Great Red Spot. Recorded over 32 days with one photograph taken every 10 hours (once per Jovian day). See <a href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_from_Voyager_1_PIA02855_max_quality.ogv" title="File:Jupiter from Voyager 1 PIA02855 max quality.ogv">full size video</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The best known feature of Jupiter is the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20171213_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20171213-64">[61]</a></sup> a persistent <a href="/wiki/Anticyclone" title="Anticyclone">anticyclonic</a> storm that is larger than Earth, located 22&#176; south of the equator. It is known to have been in existence since at least 1831,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup> and possibly since 1665.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kyrala26_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-kyrala26-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> Images by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> have shown as many as two "red spots" adjacent to the Great Red Spot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup> The storm is large enough to be visible through Earth-based <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a> with an <a href="/wiki/Aperture" title="Aperture">aperture</a> of 12&#160;cm or larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Oval" title="Oval">oval</a> object <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotates</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterclockwise" title="Counterclockwise">counterclockwise</a>, with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Period_(physics)" title="Period (physics)">period</a> of about six days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> The maximum altitude of this storm is about 8&#160;km (5&#160;mi) above the surrounding cloudtops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA14135-Jupiter-GreatRedSpot-Shrinks-20140515.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/NASA14135-Jupiter-GreatRedSpot-Shrinks-20140515.jpg/220px-NASA14135-Jupiter-GreatRedSpot-Shrinks-20140515.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/NASA14135-Jupiter-GreatRedSpot-Shrinks-20140515.jpg/330px-NASA14135-Jupiter-GreatRedSpot-Shrinks-20140515.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/NASA14135-Jupiter-GreatRedSpot-Shrinks-20140515.jpg/440px-NASA14135-Jupiter-GreatRedSpot-Shrinks-20140515.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA14135-Jupiter-GreatRedSpot-Shrinks-20140515.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> is decreasing in size (May 15, 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140515_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140515-73">[70]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The Great Red Spot is large enough to accommodate Earth within its boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sp.news20151125_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-sp.news20151125-74">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">Mathematical models</a> suggest that the storm is stable and may be a permanent feature of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> However, it has significantly decreased in size since its discovery. Initial observations in the late 1800s showed it to be approximately 41,000&#160;km (25,500&#160;mi) across. By the time of the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a></i> flybys in 1979, the storm had a length of 23,300&#160;km (14,500&#160;mi) and a width of approximately 13,000&#160;km (8,000&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon2015_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simon2015-76">[73]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a></i> observations in 1995 showed it had decreased in size again to 20,950&#160;km (13,020&#160;mi), and observations in 2009 showed the size to be 17,910&#160;km (11,130&#160;mi). As of  2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Jupiter&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the storm was measured at approximately 16,500 by 10,940&#160;km (10,250 by 6,800&#160;mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon2015_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simon2015-76">[73]</a></sup> and is decreasing in length by about 930&#160;km (580&#160;mi) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sp.news20151125_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-sp.news20151125-74">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techtimes20151021_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-techtimes20151021-77">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Storms such as this are common within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbulent" title="Turbulent">turbulent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">atmospheres</a> of <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>. Jupiter also has white ovals and brown ovals, which are lesser unnamed storms. White ovals tend to consist of relatively cool clouds within the upper atmosphere. Brown ovals are warmer and located within the "normal cloud layer". Such storms can last as little as a few hours or stretch on for centuries.\n</p><p>Even before Voyager proved that the feature was a storm, there was strong evidence that the spot could not be associated with any deeper feature on the planet\'s surface, as the Spot rotates differentially with respect to the rest of the atmosphere, sometimes faster and sometimes more slowly. \n</p><p>In 2000, an atmospheric feature formed in the southern hemisphere that is similar in appearance to the Great Red Spot, but smaller. This was created when several smaller, white oval-shaped storms merged to form a single feature&#8212;these three smaller white ovals were first observed in 1938. The merged feature was named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oval_BA" title="Oval BA">Oval BA</a>, and has been nicknamed Red Spot Junior. It has since increased in intensity and changed color from white to red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2017, scientists reported the discovery of a "Great Cold Spot" in Jupiter\'s thermosphere at its north pole that is 24,000&#160;km (15,000&#160;mi) across, 12,000&#160;km (7,500&#160;mi) wide, and 200&#160;&#176;C (360&#160;&#176;F) cooler than surrounding material. The feature was discovered by researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">Very Large Telescope</a> in Chile, who then searched archived data from the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Infrared_Telescope_Facility" title="NASA Infrared Telescope Facility">NASA Infrared Telescope Facility</a> between 1995 and 2000. They found that, while the Spot changes size, shape and intensity over the short term, it has maintained its general position in the atmosphere across more than 15 years of available data. Scientists believe the Spot is a giant vortex similar to the Great Red Spot and also appears to be <a href="/wiki/Metastability" title="Metastability">quasi-stable</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Vorticity" title="Vorticity">vortices</a> in Earth\'s thermosphere. Interactions between charged particles generated from Io and the planet\'s strong magnetic field likely resulted in redistribution of heat flow, forming the Spot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stallard_et_Al.,_2017_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stallard_et_Al.,_2017-81">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leicester20170411_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-leicester20170411-82">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yeager_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yeager-83">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thestar20170411_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-thestar20170411-84">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Magnetosphere of Jupiter">Magnetosphere of Jupiter</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:160px;max-width:160px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:157px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Captures_Vivid_Auroras_in_Jupiter%27s_Atmosphere.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2216" data-file-width="2224" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Hubble_Captures_Vivid_Auroras_in_Jupiter%27s_Atmosphere.jpg/158px-Hubble_Captures_Vivid_Auroras_in_Jupiter%27s_Atmosphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Hubble_Captures_Vivid_Auroras_in_Jupiter%27s_Atmosphere.jpg/237px-Hubble_Captures_Vivid_Auroras_in_Jupiter%27s_Atmosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Hubble_Captures_Vivid_Auroras_in_Jupiter%27s_Atmosphere.jpg/316px-Hubble_Captures_Vivid_Auroras_in_Jupiter%27s_Atmosphere.jpg 2x" width="158"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">Aurorae</a> on the north pole of Jupiter as viewed by <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:178px;max-width:178px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:157px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21033_Juno%27s_View_of_Jupiter%27s_Southern_Lights.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="733" data-file-width="822" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA21033_Juno%27s_View_of_Jupiter%27s_Southern_Lights.jpg/176px-PIA21033_Juno%27s_View_of_Jupiter%27s_Southern_Lights.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA21033_Juno%27s_View_of_Jupiter%27s_Southern_Lights.jpg/264px-PIA21033_Juno%27s_View_of_Jupiter%27s_Southern_Lights.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA21033_Juno%27s_View_of_Jupiter%27s_Southern_Lights.jpg/352px-PIA21033_Juno%27s_View_of_Jupiter%27s_Southern_Lights.jpg 2x" width="176"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">Infrared</a> view of Jupiter\'s southern lights, taken by the <a href="/wiki/Jovian_Infrared_Auroral_Mapper" title="Jovian Infrared Auroral Mapper">Jovian Infrared Auroral Mapper</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Jupiter\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> is fourteen times as strong as that of Earth, ranging from 4.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a> (0.42 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millitesla" title="Millitesla">mT</a>) at the equator to 10&#8211;14 gauss (1.0&#8211;1.4 mT) at the poles, making it the strongest in the Solar System (except for <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbook_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-worldbook-63">[60]</a></sup> This field is thought to be generated by <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">eddy currents</a>&#8212;swirling movements of conducting materials&#8212;within the liquid metallic hydrogen core. The volcanoes on the moon <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> emit large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> forming a gas torus along the moon\'s orbit. The gas is ionized in the magnetosphere producing <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>. They, together with hydrogen ions originating from the atmosphere of Jupiter, form a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_sheet" title="Plasma sheet">plasma sheet</a> in Jupiter\'s equatorial plane. The plasma in the sheet co-rotates with the planet causing deformation of the dipole magnetic field into that of magnetodisk. Electrons within the plasma sheet generate a strong radio signature that produces bursts in the range of 0.6&#8211;30&#160;<a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">MHz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At about 75 Jupiter radii from the planet, the interaction of the magnetosphere with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> generates a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a>. Surrounding Jupiter\'s magnetosphere is a <a href="/wiki/Magnetopause" title="Magnetopause">magnetopause</a>, located at the inner edge of a <a href="/wiki/Magnetosheath" title="Magnetosheath">magnetosheath</a>&#8212;a region between it and the bow shock. The solar wind interacts with these regions, elongating the magnetosphere on Jupiter\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lee_side" title="Lee side">lee side</a> and extending it outward until it nearly reaches the orbit of Saturn. The four largest moons of Jupiter all orbit within the magnetosphere, which protects them from the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-6"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The magnetosphere of Jupiter is responsible for intense episodes of <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio emission</a> from the planet\'s polar regions. Volcanic activity on Jupiter\'s moon Io (see below) injects gas into Jupiter\'s magnetosphere, producing a torus of particles about the planet. As Io moves through this torus, the interaction generates <a href="/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n_wave" title="Alfv&#233;n wave">Alfv&#233;n waves</a> that carry ionized matter into the polar regions of Jupiter. As a result, radio waves are generated through a <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a> <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_maser" title="Astrophysical maser">maser mechanism</a>, and the energy is transmitted out along a cone-shaped surface. When Earth intersects this cone, the radio emissions from Jupiter can exceed the solar radio output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="248" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif/220px-Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/08/Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solarsystem3DJupiter.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter (red) completes one orbit of the Sun (center) for every 11.86 orbits of Earth (blue)</div></div></div>\n<p>Jupiter is the only planet whose <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a> with the Sun lies outside the volume of the Sun, though by only 7% of the Sun\'s radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> The average distance between Jupiter and the Sun is 778 million&#160;km (about 5.2 times the average distance between Earth and the Sun, or 5.2 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>) and it completes an orbit every 11.86&#160;years. This is approximately two-fifths the orbital period of Saturn, forming a near <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> between the two largest planets in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> The elliptical orbit of Jupiter is inclined 1.31&#176; compared to Earth. Because the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> of its orbit is 0.048, Jupiter\'s distance from the Sun varies by 75 million&#160;km between its nearest approach (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>) and furthest distance (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a>).\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> of Jupiter is relatively small: only 3.13&#176;. As a result, it does not experience significant seasonal changes, in contrast to, for example, Earth and Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jupiter\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Period_of_revolution" title="Period of revolution">rotation</a> is the fastest of all the Solar System\'s planets, completing a rotation on its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinate_axis" title="Coordinate axis">axis</a> in slightly less than ten hours; this creates an <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_bulge" title="Equatorial bulge">equatorial bulge</a> easily seen through an Earth-based amateur <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>. The planet is shaped as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroid</a>, meaning that the diameter across its <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> is longer than the diameter measured between its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographic_pole" title="Geographic pole">poles</a>. On Jupiter, the equatorial diameter is 9,275&#160;km (5,763&#160;mi) longer than the diameter measured through the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang03_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-lang03-56">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because Jupiter is not a solid body, its upper atmosphere undergoes <a href="/wiki/Differential_rotation" title="Differential rotation">differential rotation</a>. The rotation of Jupiter\'s polar atmosphere is about 5&#160;minutes longer than that of the equatorial atmosphere; three systems are used as frames of reference, particularly when graphing the motion of atmospheric features. System I applies from the latitudes 10&#176;&#160;N to 10&#176;&#160;S; its period is the planet\'s shortest, at 9h 50m 30.0s. System II applies at all latitudes north and south of these; its period is 9h 55m 40.6s. System III was first defined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_astronomer" title="Radio astronomer">radio astronomers</a>, and corresponds to the rotation of the planet\'s magnetosphere; its period is Jupiter\'s official rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span></h2>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:163px;max-width:163px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:107px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conjunction_of_Jupiter_and_Moon.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Conjunction_of_Jupiter_and_Moon.jpg/161px-Conjunction_of_Jupiter_and_Moon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Conjunction_of_Jupiter_and_Moon.jpg/242px-Conjunction_of_Jupiter_and_Moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Conjunction_of_Jupiter_and_Moon.jpg/322px-Conjunction_of_Jupiter_and_Moon.jpg 2x" width="161"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Conjunction of Jupiter and the Moon</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:175px;max-width:175px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:107px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Retrogradation1.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="539" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Retrogradation1.svg/173px-Retrogradation1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Retrogradation1.svg/260px-Retrogradation1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Retrogradation1.svg/346px-Retrogradation1.svg.png 2x" width="173"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The retrograde motion of an outer planet is caused by its relative location with respect to Earth</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Jupiter is usually the fourth brightest object in the sky (after the Sun, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbook_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-worldbook-63">[60]</a></sup> at times <a href="/wiki/Mars#Viewing" title="Mars">Mars</a> appears brighter than Jupiter. Depending on Jupiter\'s position with respect to the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, it can vary in visual magnitude from as bright as &#8722;2.94<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-15">[12]</a></sup> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_(astronomy)" title="Opposition (astronomy)">opposition</a> down to<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-15">[12]</a></sup> &#8722;1.66 during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology)" title="Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)">conjunction</a> with the Sun. The mean apparent magnitude is -2.20 with a standard deviation of 0.33.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-15">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular diameter</a> of Jupiter likewise varies from 50.1 to 29.8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arc_second" title="Arc second">arc seconds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-fact-7">[6]</a></sup> Favorable oppositions occur when Jupiter is passing through <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a>, an event that occurs once per orbit.\n</p><p>Earth overtakes Jupiter every 398.9 days as it orbits the Sun, a duration called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synodic_period" title="Synodic period">synodic period</a>. As it does so, Jupiter appears to undergo <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">retrograde motion</a> with respect to the background stars. That is, for a period Jupiter seems to move backward in the night sky, performing a looping motion.\n</p><p>Because the orbit of Jupiter is outside that of Earth, the <a href="/wiki/Phase_angle_(astronomy)" title="Phase angle (astronomy)">phase angle</a> of Jupiter as viewed from Earth never exceeds 11.5&#176;. That is, the planet always appears nearly fully illuminated when viewed through Earth-based telescopes. It was only during spacecraft missions to Jupiter that crescent views of the planet were obtained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup> A small telescope will usually show Jupiter\'s four <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> and the prominent cloud belts across <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">Jupiter\'s atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> A large telescope will show Jupiter\'s <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> when it faces Earth.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter-bonatti.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="572" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Jupiter-bonatti.png/220px-Jupiter-bonatti.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Jupiter-bonatti.png/330px-Jupiter-bonatti.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Jupiter-bonatti.png/440px-Jupiter-bonatti.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter-bonatti.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter, woodcut from a 1550 edition of <a href="/wiki/Guido_Bonatti" title="Guido Bonatti">Guido Bonatti</a>\'s <i>Liber Astronomiae</i></div></div></div>\n<p>The planet Jupiter has been known since ancient times. It is visible to the naked eye in the night sky and can occasionally be seen in the daytime when the Sun is low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup> To the <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylonians</a>, this object represented their god <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a>. They used Jupiter\'s roughly 12-year orbit along the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> to define the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellations</a> of their <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">zodiac</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-36">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Romans called it "the star of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>" (<i>Iuppiter Stella</i>), as they believed it to be sacred to the principal <a href="/wiki/God_(male_deity)" title="God (male deity)">god</a> of <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a>, whose name comes from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vocative" title="Vocative">vocative</a> compound *<i>Dy&#275;u-p&#601;ter</i> (nominative: *<i><a href="/wiki/Dyeus" title="Dyeus">Dy&#275;us</a>-p&#601;t&#275;r</i>, meaning "Father Sky-God", or "Father Day-God").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymologyonline_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymologyonline-95">[92]</a></sup> In turn, Jupiter was the counterpart to the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">mythical Greek</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a></i> (&#918;&#949;&#973;&#962;), also referred to as <i>Dias</i> (&#916;&#943;&#945;&#962;), the planetary name of which is retained in modern <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> The ancient Greeks knew the planet as <a href="/wiki/Phaethon" title="Phaethon">Phaethon</a>, meaning "shining one" or "blazing star."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> As supreme god of the Roman pantheon, Jupiter was the god of thunder, lightning and storms, and appropriately called the god of light and sky.\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbol" title="Astronomical symbol">astronomical symbol</a> for the planet, <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_symbol.svg"><img alt="Jupiter symbol.svg" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Jupiter_symbol.svg/14px-Jupiter_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Jupiter_symbol.svg/21px-Jupiter_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Jupiter_symbol.svg/28px-Jupiter_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="14"/></a>, is a stylized representation of the god\'s lightning bolt. The original Greek deity <i>Zeus</i> supplies the root <i>zeno-</i>, used to form some Jupiter-related words, such as <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/zenographic" title="wikt:zenographic">zenographic</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[d]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Jovian</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectival</a> form of Jupiter. The older adjectival form <i>jovial</i>, employed by astrologers in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, has come to mean "happy" or "merry", moods ascribed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_(astrology)" title="Jupiter (astrology)">Jupiter\'s astrological influence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Chinese, Vietnamese, Koreans and Japanese called it the "wood star" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">&#26408;&#26143;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">m&#249;x&#299;ng</span></i>), based on the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_elements_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Five elements (Chinese philosophy)">Five Elements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup> Chinese Taoism personified it as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fu_star" title="Fu star">Fu star</a>. The Greeks called it <i>&#934;&#945;&#941;&#952;&#969;&#957;</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Phaethon" title="Phaethon">Phaethon</a></i>, meaning "blazing").\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Jyotisha" title="Jyotisha">Vedic astrology</a>, Hindu astrologers named the planet after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brihaspati" title="Brihaspati">Brihaspati</a>, the religious teacher of the gods, and often called it "<a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">Guru</a>", which literally means the "Heavy One".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic mythology</a>, Jupiter is equated to <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>, whence the English name <i>Thursday</i> for the Roman <i>dies Jovis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Altaic_mythologies" title="Altaic mythologies">Central Asian-Turkic myths</a>, Jupiter is called <i>Erendiz</i> or <i>Erent&#252;z</i>, from <i>eren</i> (of uncertain meaning) and <i>yultuz</i> ("star"). There are many theories about the meaning of <i>eren</i>. These peoples calculated the period of the orbit of Jupiter as 11 years and 300 days. They believed that some social and natural events connected to Erent&#252;z\'s movements on the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_research_and_exploration">History of research and exploration</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-telescopic_research">Pre-telescopic research</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Almagest-planets.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Almagest-planets.svg/220px-Almagest-planets.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Almagest-planets.svg/330px-Almagest-planets.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Almagest-planets.svg/440px-Almagest-planets.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Almagest-planets.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model in the <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i> of the longitudinal motion of Jupiter (&#9737;) relative to Earth (&#8853;)</div></div></div>\n<p>The observation of Jupiter dates back to at least the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy" title="Babylonian astronomy">Babylonian astronomers</a> of the 7th or 8th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup> The ancient Chinese also observed the orbit of <i>Su&#236;x&#299;ng</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%AD%B2%E6%98%9F" title="wikt:&#27506;&#26143;">&#27506;&#26143;</a></span>) and established their cycle of 12 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earthly_branches" title="Earthly branches">earthly branches</a> based on its approximate number of years; the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese language</a> still uses its name (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_characters" title="Simplified characters">simplified</a> as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%B2%81" title="wikt:&#23681;">&#23681;</a></span>) when referring to years of age. By the 4th century BC, these observations had developed into the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_zodiac" title="Chinese zodiac">Chinese zodiac</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup> with each year associated with a <a href="/wiki/Tai_Sui" title="Tai Sui">Tai Sui</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">star</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_gods" title="Chinese gods">god</a> controlling the region of the heavens opposite Jupiter\'s position in the night sky; these beliefs survive in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist" title="Taoist">Taoist</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">religious practices</a> and in the East Asian zodiac\'s twelve animals, now often <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">popularly assumed</a> to be related to the arrival of the animals before <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Buddha</a>. The Chinese historian <a href="/wiki/Xi_Zezong" title="Xi Zezong">Xi Zezong</a> has claimed that <a href="/wiki/Gan_De" title="Gan De">Gan De</a>, an ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomer</a>, discovered one of <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">Jupiter\'s moons</a> in 362&#160;BC with the unaided eye. If accurate, this would predate Galileo\'s discovery by nearly two millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup> In his 2nd century work the <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>, the Hellenistic astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemaeus" title="Claudius Ptolemaeus">Claudius Ptolemaeus</a> constructed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentric" title="Geocentric">geocentric</a> planetary model based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deferent" title="Deferent">deferents</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicycle" title="Epicycle">epicycles</a> to explain Jupiter\'s motion relative to Earth, giving its orbital period around Earth as 4332.38&#160;days, or 11.86&#160;years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground-based_telescope_research">Ground-based telescope research</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="1180" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/180px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/270px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/360px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, discoverer of the four moons of Jupiter, now known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_moon" title="Galilean moon">Galilean moons</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1610, Italian polymath <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> discovered the four largest <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> of Jupiter (now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_moon" title="Galilean moon">Galilean moons</a>) using a telescope; thought to be the first telescopic observation of moons other than Earth\'s. One day after Galileo, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a> independently discovered moons around Jupiter, though he did not publish his discovery in a book until 1614.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup> It was Marius\'s names for the four major moons, however, that stuck&#8212;Io, Europa, Ganymede and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>. These findings were also the first discovery of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_mechanics" title="Celestial mechanics">celestial motion</a> not apparently centered on Earth. The discovery was a major point in favor of <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernicus\'</a> <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric</a> theory of the motions of the planets; Galileo\'s outspoken support of the Copernican theory placed him under the threat of the <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">Inquisition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1660s, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Cassini</a> used a new telescope to discover spots and colorful bands on Jupiter and observed that the planet appeared oblate; that is, flattened at the poles. He was also able to estimate the rotation period of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cassini1_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-cassini1-114">[110]</a></sup> In 1690 Cassini noticed that the atmosphere undergoes <a href="/wiki/Differential_rotation" title="Differential rotation">differential rotation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-7"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Great Red Spot, a prominent oval-shaped feature in the southern hemisphere of Jupiter, may have been observed as early as 1664 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> and in 1665 by Cassini, although this is disputed. The pharmacist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_Heinrich_Schwabe" title="Samuel Heinrich Schwabe">Heinrich Schwabe</a> produced the earliest known drawing to show details of the Great Red Spot in 1831.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Red Spot was reportedly lost from sight on several occasions between 1665 and 1708 before becoming quite conspicuous in 1878. It was recorded as fading again in 1883 and at the start of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Alfonso_Borelli" title="Giovanni Alfonso Borelli">Giovanni Borelli</a> and Cassini made careful tables of the motions of Jupiter\'s moons, allowing predictions of the times when the moons would pass before or behind the planet. By the 1670s, it was observed that when Jupiter was on the opposite side of the Sun from Earth, these events would occur about 17&#160;minutes later than expected. <a href="/wiki/Ole_R%C3%B8mer" title="Ole R&#248;mer">Ole R&#248;mer</a> deduced that light does not travel instantaneously (a conclusion that Cassini had earlier rejected),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cassini_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-cassini-30">[27]</a></sup> and this timing discrepancy was used to estimate the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1892, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._E._Barnard" title="E. E. Barnard">E. E. Barnard</a> observed a fifth satellite of Jupiter with the 36-inch (910&#160;mm) refractor at <a href="/wiki/Lick_Observatory" title="Lick Observatory">Lick Observatory</a> in California. The discovery of this relatively small object, a testament to his keen eyesight, quickly made him famous.
