This moon was later named <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup> It was the last planetary moon to be discovered directly by visual observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[115]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_MAD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1730" data-file-width="1730" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Jupiter_MAD.jpg/220px-Jupiter_MAD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Jupiter_MAD.jpg/330px-Jupiter_MAD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Jupiter_MAD.jpg/440px-Jupiter_MAD.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_MAD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Infrared image of Jupiter taken by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESO" title="ESO">ESO</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">Very Large Telescope</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1932, <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Wildt" title="Rupert Wildt">Rupert Wildt</a> identified absorption bands of ammonia and methane in the spectra of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Three long-lived anticyclonic features termed white ovals were observed in 1938. For several decades they remained as separate features in the atmosphere, sometimes approaching each other but never merging. Finally, two of the ovals merged in 1998, then absorbed the third in 2000, becoming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oval_BA" title="Oval BA">Oval BA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiotelescope_research">Radiotelescope research</span></h3>\n<p>In 1955, Bernard Burke and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Franklin" title="Kenneth Franklin">Kenneth Franklin</a> detected bursts of radio signals coming from Jupiter at 22.2&#160;MHz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-8"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup> The period of these bursts matched the rotation of the planet, and they were also able to use this information to refine the rotation rate. Radio bursts from Jupiter were found to come in two forms: long bursts (or L-bursts) lasting up to several seconds, and short bursts (or S-bursts) that had a duration of less than a hundredth of a second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scientists discovered that there were three forms of radio signals transmitted from Jupiter.\n</p>\n<ul><li>Decametric radio bursts (with a wavelength of tens of meters) vary with the rotation of Jupiter, and are influenced by interaction of Io with Jupiter\'s magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Decimetric radio emission (with wavelengths measured in centimeters) was first observed by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Drake" title="Frank Drake">Frank Drake</a> and Hein Hvatum in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-9"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup> The origin of this signal was from a torus-shaped belt around Jupiter\'s equator. This signal is caused by <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron_radiation" title="Cyclotron radiation">cyclotron radiation</a> from electrons that are accelerated in Jupiter\'s magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Thermal radiation is produced by heat in the atmosphere of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-10"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Jupiter" title="Exploration of Jupiter">Exploration of Jupiter</a></div>\n<p>Since 1973 a number of automated spacecraft have visited Jupiter, most notably the <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i> space probe, the first spacecraft to get close enough to Jupiter to send back revelations about the properties and phenomena of the Solar System\'s largest planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup> Flights to other planets within the Solar System are accomplished at a cost in energy, which is described by the net change in velocity of the spacecraft, or <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a>. Entering a <a href="/wiki/Hohmann_transfer_orbit" title="Hohmann transfer orbit">Hohmann transfer orbit</a> from Earth to Jupiter from <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a> requires a delta-v of 6.3&#160;km/s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup> which is comparable to the 9.7&#160;km/s delta-v needed to reach low Earth orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[124]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">Gravity assists</a> through planetary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">flybys</a> can be used to reduce the energy required to reach Jupiter, albeit at the cost of a significantly longer flight duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delta-v_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-delta-v-129">[125]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flyby_missions">Flyby missions</span></h4>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21645-Jupiter-PerijovePass-JunoCam-20170525.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4320" data-file-width="23520" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/PIA21645-Jupiter-PerijovePass-JunoCam-20170525.jpg/800px-PIA21645-Jupiter-PerijovePass-JunoCam-20170525.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/PIA21645-Jupiter-PerijovePass-JunoCam-20170525.jpg/1200px-PIA21645-Jupiter-PerijovePass-JunoCam-20170525.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/PIA21645-Jupiter-PerijovePass-JunoCam-20170525.jpg/1600px-PIA21645-Jupiter-PerijovePass-JunoCam-20170525.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA21645-Jupiter-PerijovePass-JunoCam-20170525.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Perijove 6 pass of Jupiter as viewed by <a href="/wiki/JunoCam" title="JunoCam">JunoCam</a></center></div></div></div></div>\n<table class="wikitable floatright">\n<caption>Flyby missions\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Spacecraft\n</th>\n<th>Closest <br/>approach\n</th>\n<th>Distance\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i>\n</td>\n<td>December 3, 1973\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">130,000&#160;km\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i>\n</td>\n<td>December 4, 1974\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">34,000&#160;km\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i>\n</td>\n<td>March 5, 1979\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">349,000&#160;km\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i>\n</td>\n<td>July 9, 1979\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">570,000&#160;km\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulysses_probe" title="Ulysses probe">Ulysses</a></i>\n</td>\n<td>February 8, 1992<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ulysses_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-ulysses-130">[126]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">408,894&#160;km\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>February 4, 2004<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ulysses_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-ulysses-130">[126]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">120,000,000&#160;km\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i>\n</td>\n<td>December 30, 2000\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">10,000,000&#160;km\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i>\n</td>\n<td>February 28, 2007\n</td>\n<td style="text-align: right;">2,304,535&#160;km\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Beginning in 1973, several spacecraft have performed planetary flyby maneuvers that brought them within observation range of Jupiter. The <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_program" title="Pioneer program">Pioneer</a> missions obtained the first close-up images of Jupiter\'s atmosphere and several of its moons. They discovered that the radiation fields near the planet were much stronger than expected, but both spacecraft managed to survive in that environment. The trajectories of these spacecraft were used to refine the mass estimates of the Jovian system. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_occultations" title="Radio occultations">Radio occultations</a> by the planet resulted in better measurements of Jupiter\'s diameter and the amount of polar flattening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-36">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmology_101_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosmology_101-131">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Six years later, the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> missions vastly improved the understanding of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_moon" title="Galilean moon">Galilean moons</a> and discovered Jupiter\'s rings. They also confirmed that the Great Red Spot was anticyclonic. Comparison of images showed that the Red Spot had changed hue since the Pioneer missions, turning from orange to dark brown. A torus of ionized atoms was discovered along Io\'s orbital path, and volcanoes were found on the moon\'s surface, some in the process of erupting. As the spacecraft passed behind the planet, it observed flashes of lightning in the night side atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-36">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voyager1_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-voyager1-132">[128]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The next mission to encounter Jupiter was the <i>Ulysses</i> solar probe. It performed a flyby maneuver to attain a <a href="/wiki/Polar_orbit" title="Polar orbit">polar orbit</a> around the Sun. During this pass, the spacecraft conducted studies on Jupiter\'s magnetosphere. <i>Ulysses</i> has no cameras so no images were taken. A second flyby six years later was at a much greater distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ulysses_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-ulysses-130">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA02879_-_A_New_Year_for_Jupiter_and_Io.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="999" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/PIA02879_-_A_New_Year_for_Jupiter_and_Io.jpg/220px-PIA02879_-_A_New_Year_for_Jupiter_and_Io.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/PIA02879_-_A_New_Year_for_Jupiter_and_Io.jpg/330px-PIA02879_-_A_New_Year_for_Jupiter_and_Io.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/PIA02879_-_A_New_Year_for_Jupiter_and_Io.jpg/440px-PIA02879_-_A_New_Year_for_Jupiter_and_Io.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA02879_-_A_New_Year_for_Jupiter_and_Io.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Cassini</i> views Jupiter and Io on January 1, 2001</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2000, the <i>Cassini</i> probe flew by Jupiter on its way to <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, and provided some of the highest-resolution images ever made of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> probe flew by Jupiter for a gravity assist en route to <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>. Its closest approach was on February 28, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[130]</a></sup> The probe\'s cameras measured plasma output from volcanoes on Io and studied all four Galilean moons in detail, as well as making long-distance observations of the outer moons <a href="/wiki/Himalia_(moon)" title="Himalia (moon)">Himalia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elara_(moon)" title="Elara (moon)">Elara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[131]</a></sup> Imaging of the Jovian system began September 4, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Galileo_mission"><i>Galileo</i> mission</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo (spacecraft)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Jupiter_from_Cassini.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3207" data-file-width="2260" decoding="async" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Portrait_of_Jupiter_from_Cassini.jpg/220px-Portrait_of_Jupiter_from_Cassini.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Portrait_of_Jupiter_from_Cassini.jpg/330px-Portrait_of_Jupiter_from_Cassini.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Portrait_of_Jupiter_from_Cassini.jpg/440px-Portrait_of_Jupiter_from_Cassini.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Jupiter_from_Cassini.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter as seen by the space probe <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>The first spacecraft to orbit Jupiter was the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo_spacecraft" title="Galileo spacecraft">Galileo</a></i> probe, which entered orbit on December 7, 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-41">[38]</a></sup> It orbited the planet for over seven years, conducting multiple flybys of all the Galilean moons and <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a>. The spacecraft also witnessed the impact of <a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9</a> as it approached Jupiter in 1994, giving a unique vantage point for the event. Its originally designed capacity was limited by the failed deployment of its high-gain radio antenna, although extensive information was still gained about the Jovian system from <i>Galileo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galileo_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-galileo-138">[134]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 340-kilogram titanium <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)#Galileo_Probe" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">atmospheric probe</a> was released from the spacecraft in July 1995, entering Jupiter\'s atmosphere on December 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-41">[38]</a></sup> It parachuted through 150&#160;km (93&#160;mi) of the atmosphere at a speed of about 2,575&#160;km/h (1600&#160;mph)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-41">[38]</a></sup> and collected data for 57.6&#160;minutes before the signal was lost at a pressure of about 23 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(pressure)" title="Atmosphere (pressure)">atmospheres</a> at a temperature of 153&#160;&#176;C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[135]</a></sup> It melted thereafter, and possibly vaporized. The <i>Galileo</i> orbiter itself experienced a more rapid version of the same fate when it was deliberately steered into the planet on September 21, 2003 at a speed of over 50&#160;km/s to avoid any possibility of it crashing into and possibly contaminating Europa, a moon which has been hypothesized to have the possibility of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_on_Europa" title="Life on Europa">harboring life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galileo_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-galileo-138">[134]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Data from this mission revealed that hydrogen composes up to 90% of Jupiter\'s atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-41">[38]</a></sup> The recorded temperature was more than 300&#160;&#176;C (&gt;570&#160;&#176;F) and the windspeed measured more than 644&#160;km/h (&gt;400&#160;mph) before the probes vapourised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_41-5"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-41">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Juno_mission"><i>Juno</i> mission</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno (spacecraft)</a></div>\n<p>NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)"><i>Juno</i></a> mission arrived at Jupiter on July 4, 2016, and is expected to complete 37 orbits over the next 20 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160705_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160705-25">[22]</a></sup> The mission plan called for <i>Juno</i> to study the planet in detail from a <a href="/wiki/Polar_orbit" title="Polar orbit">polar orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[136]</a></sup> On August 27, 2016, the spacecraft completed its first fly-by of Jupiter and sent back the first-ever images of Jupiter&#8217;s north pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_probes">Future probes</span></h4>\n<p>The next planned mission to the Jovian system will be the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moon_Explorer" title="Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer">Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer</a> (JUICE), due to launch in 2022,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selection_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-selection-142">[138]</a></sup> followed by NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Europa_Clipper" title="Europa Clipper">Europa Clipper</a> mission in 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[139]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canceled_missions">Canceled missions</span></h4>\n<p>There has been great interest in studying the icy moons in detail because of the possibility of subsurface liquid oceans on Jupiter\'s moons Europa, Ganymede, and Callisto. Funding difficulties have delayed progress. NASA\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moons_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Icy Moons Orbiter">JIMO</a></i> (<i>Jupiter Icy Moons Orbiter</i>) was cancelled in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[140]</a></sup> A subsequent proposal was developed for a joint <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">ESA</a> mission called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EJSM/Laplace" title="EJSM/Laplace">EJSM/Laplace</a>, with a provisional launch date around 2020. EJSM/Laplace would have consisted of the NASA-led <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Europa_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Europa Orbiter">Jupiter Europa Orbiter</a> and the ESA-led <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Ganymede_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Ganymede Orbiter">Jupiter Ganymede Orbiter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup> However, ESA had formally ended the partnership by April 2011, citing budget issues at NASA and the consequences on the mission timetable. Instead, ESA planned to go ahead with a European-only mission to compete in its L1 <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Vision" title="Cosmic Vision">Cosmic Vision</a> selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esaled_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-esaled-146">[142]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moons">Moons</span></h2>\n<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/30px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/45px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/59px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikimedia Commons has media related to <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Moons_of_Jupiter" title="commons:Category:Moons of Jupiter">Moons of Jupiter</a></b></i>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">Moons of Jupiter</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_discovery_of_Solar_System_planets_and_their_moons" title="Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons">Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons</a></div>\n<p>Jupiter has 79 known <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79moons_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-79moons-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shep-main_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-shep-main-147">[143]</a></sup> Of these, 63 are less than 10 kilometres in diameter and have only been discovered since 1975. The four largest moons, visible from Earth with binoculars on a clear night, known as the "<a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>", are Io, Europa, Ganymede, and Callisto.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galilean_moons">Galilean moons</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a></div>\n<p>The moons discovered by Galileo&#8212;Io, Europa, Ganymede, and Callisto&#8212;are among the largest satellites in the Solar System. The orbits of three of them (Io, Europa, and Ganymede) form a pattern known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laplace_resonance" title="Laplace resonance">Laplace resonance</a>; for every four orbits that Io makes around Jupiter, Europa makes exactly two orbits and Ganymede makes exactly one. This resonance causes the <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational</a> effects of the three large moons to distort their orbits into elliptical shapes, because each moon receives an extra tug from its neighbors at the same point in every orbit it makes. The <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal force</a> from Jupiter, on the other hand, works to <a href="/wiki/Tidal_circularization" title="Tidal circularization">circularize</a> their orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[144]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> of their orbits causes regular flexing of the three moons\' shapes, with Jupiter\'s gravity stretching them out as they approach it and allowing them to spring back to more spherical shapes as they swing away. This tidal flexing <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration#Tidal_heating" title="Tidal acceleration">heats</a> the moons\' interiors by <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a>. This is seen most dramatically in the extraordinary <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)#Volcanism" title="Io (moon)">volcanic activity</a> of innermost Io (which is subject to the strongest tidal forces), and to a lesser degree in the geological youth of <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)#Surface_features" title="Europa (moon)">Europa\'s surface</a> (indicating recent resurfacing of the moon\'s exterior).\n</p>\n<table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="width:550px; margin:0 auto;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:right; margin:0 auto;">\n<caption>The Galilean moons, compared to Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th rowspan="2">Name\n</th>\n<th rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">IPA</a>\n</th>\n<th colspan="2">Diameter\n</th>\n<th colspan="2">Mass\n</th>\n<th colspan="2">Orbital radius\n</th>\n<th colspan="2">Orbital period\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>km\n</th>\n<th>%\n</th>\n<th>kg\n</th>\n<th>%\n</th>\n<th>km\n</th>\n<th>%\n</th>\n<th>days\n</th>\n<th>%\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (IPA)">/&#712;a&#618;.o&#650;/</span></span>\n</td>\n<td>3,643\n</td>\n<td>105\n</td>\n<td>8.9&#215;10<sup>22</sup>\n</td>\n<td>120\n</td>\n<td>421,700\n</td>\n<td>110\n</td>\n<td>1.77\n</td>\n<td>7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>\n</th>\n<td align="left"><span style="font-size:85%;"><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/&#712;&#609;&#230;nimi&#720;d/</span></span>\n</td>\n<td>5,262\n</td>\n<td>150\n</td>\n<td>14.8&#215;10<sup>22</sup>\n</td>\n<td>200\n</td>\n<td>1,070,412\n</td>\n<td>280\n</td>\n<td>7.15\n</td>\n<td>26\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>\n</th>\n<td align="left"><span style="font-size:85%;"><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k&#601;&#712;l&#618;sto&#650;/</span></span>\n</td>\n<td>4,821\n</td>\n<td>140\n</td>\n<td>10.8&#215;10<sup>22</sup>\n</td>\n<td>150\n</td>\n<td>1,882,709\n</td>\n<td>490\n</td>\n<td>16.69\n</td>\n<td>61\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Galilean_satellites_(the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter).tif" title="The Galilean moons. From left to right, in order of increasing distance from Jupiter: Io, Europa, Ganymede, Callisto."><img alt="The Galilean moons. From left to right, in order of increasing distance from Jupiter: Io, Europa, Ganymede, Callisto." data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1830" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif/lossy-page1-561px-The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif/lossy-page1-842px-The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif/lossy-page1-1122px-The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif.jpg 2x" width="561"/></a></div></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="font-size:0.9em; text-align:center;">The Galilean moons <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a> (in order of increasing distance from Jupiter)\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>\n<p>Before the discoveries of the Voyager missions, Jupiter\'s moons were arranged neatly into four groups of four, based on commonality of their <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbital elements</a>. Since then, the large number of new small outer moons has complicated this picture. There are now thought to be six main groups, although some are more distinct than others.\n</p><p>A basic sub-division is a grouping of the eight inner regular moons, which have nearly circular orbits near the plane of Jupiter\'s equator and are thought to have formed with Jupiter. The remainder of the moons consist of an unknown number of small irregular moons with elliptical and inclined orbits, which are thought to be captured asteroids or fragments of captured asteroids. Irregular moons that belong to a group share similar orbital elements and thus may have a common origin, perhaps as a larger moon or captured body that broke up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[146]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">Regular moons\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_satellites_of_Jupiter" title="Inner satellites of Jupiter">Inner group</a>\n</td>\n<td>The inner group of four small moons all have diameters of less than 200&#160;km, orbit at radii less than 200,000&#160;km, and have orbital inclinations of less than half a degree.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean&#160;moons</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[147]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td>These four moons, discovered by <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a> in parallel, orbit between 400,000 and 2,000,000&#160;km, and are some of the largest moons in the Solar System.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2">Irregular moons\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Themisto_(moon)" title="Themisto (moon)">Themisto</a>\n</td>\n<td>This is a single moon belonging to a group of its own, orbiting halfway between the Galilean moons and the Himalia group.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Himalia_group" title="Himalia group">Himalia group</a>\n</td>\n<td>A tightly clustered group of moons with orbits around 11,000,000&#8211;12,000,000&#160;km from Jupiter.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Carpo_(moon)" title="Carpo (moon)">Carpo</a>\n</td>\n<td>Another isolated case; at the inner edge of the Ananke group, it orbits Jupiter in prograde direction.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Valetudo_(moon)" title="Valetudo (moon)">Valetudo</a>\n</td>\n<td>A third isolated case, which has a prograde orbit but overlaps the retrograde groups listed below; this may result in a future collision.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ananke_group" title="Ananke group">Ananke&#160;group</a>\n</td>\n<td>This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">retrograde orbit</a> group has rather indistinct borders, averaging 21,276,000&#160;km from Jupiter with an average inclination of 149 degrees.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Carme_group" title="Carme group">Carme group</a>\n</td>\n<td>A fairly distinct retrograde group that averages 23,404,000&#160;km from Jupiter with an average inclination of 165 degrees.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pasiphae_group" title="Pasiphae group">Pasiphae group</a>\n</td>\n<td>A dispersed and only vaguely distinct retrograde group that covers all the outermost moons.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_rings">Planetary rings</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA01627_Ringe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PIA01627_Ringe.jpg/220px-PIA01627_Ringe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PIA01627_Ringe.jpg/330px-PIA01627_Ringe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PIA01627_Ringe.jpg/440px-PIA01627_Ringe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA01627_Ringe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Jupiter" title="Rings of Jupiter">rings of Jupiter</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Jupiter" title="Rings of Jupiter">Rings of Jupiter</a></div>\n<p>Jupiter has a faint <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">planetary ring</a> system composed of three main segments: an inner <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">torus</a> of particles known as the halo, a relatively bright main ring, and an outer gossamer ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[148]</a></sup> These rings appear to be made of dust, rather than ice as with Saturn\'s rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_45-11"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-45">[42]</a></sup> The main ring is probably made of material ejected from the satellites <a href="/wiki/Adrastea_(moon)" title="Adrastea (moon)">Adrastea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metis_(moon)" title="Metis (moon)">Metis</a>. Material that would normally fall back to the moon is pulled into Jupiter because of its strong gravitational influence. The orbit of the material veers towards Jupiter and new material is added by additional impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns1999_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burns1999-153">[149]</a></sup> In a similar way, the moons <a href="/wiki/Thebe_(moon)" title="Thebe (moon)">Thebe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a> probably produce the two distinct components of the dusty gossamer ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns1999_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burns1999-153">[149]</a></sup> There is also evidence of a rocky ring strung along Amalthea\'s orbit which may consist of collisional debris from that moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[150]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction_with_the_Solar_System">Interaction with the Solar System</span></h2>\n<p>Along with the Sun, the <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational</a> influence of Jupiter has helped shape the Solar System. The orbits of most of the system\'s planets lie closer to Jupiter\'s <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">orbital plane</a> than the Sun\'s <a href="/wiki/Celestial_equator" title="Celestial equator">equatorial plane</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> is the only planet that is closer to the Sun\'s equator in orbital tilt), the <a href="/wiki/Kirkwood_gap" title="Kirkwood gap">Kirkwood gaps</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> are mostly caused by Jupiter, and the planet may have been responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a> of the inner Solar System\'s history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[151]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/220px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/330px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/440px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This diagram shows the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_(astronomy)" title="Trojan (astronomy)">Trojan asteroids</a> in Jupiter\'s orbit, as well as the main <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Along with its moons, Jupiter\'s gravitational field controls numerous <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> that have settled into the regions of the <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a> preceding and following Jupiter in its orbit around the Sun. These are known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroid" title="Trojan asteroid">Trojan asteroids</a>, and are divided into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Trojan_asteroids_(Greek_camp)" title="List of Trojan asteroids (Greek camp)">Greek</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Trojan_asteroids_(Trojan_camp)" title="List of Trojan asteroids (Trojan camp)">Trojan</a> "camps" to commemorate the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>. The first of these, <a href="/wiki/588_Achilles" title="588 Achilles">588 Achilles</a>, was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Max_Wolf" title="Max Wolf">Max Wolf</a> in 1906; since then more than two thousand have been discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[152]</a></sup> The largest is <a href="/wiki/624_Hektor" title="624 Hektor">624 Hektor</a>.\n</p><p>Most <a href="/wiki/List_of_periodic_comets" title="List of periodic comets">short-period comets</a> belong to the Jupiter family&#8212;defined as comets with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axes</a> smaller than Jupiter\'s. Jupiter family comets are thought to form in the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> outside the orbit of Neptune. During close encounters with Jupiter their orbits are <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbed</a> into a smaller period and then circularized by regular gravitational interaction with the Sun and Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[153]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to the magnitude of Jupiter\'s mass, the center of gravity between it and the Sun lies just above the Sun\'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jupiter-COM-20160730_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jupiter-COM-20160730-158">[154]</a></sup> Jupiter is the only body in the Solar System for which this is true.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts">Impacts</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9</a>, <a href="/wiki/2009_Jupiter_impact_event" title="2009 Jupiter impact event">2009 Jupiter impact event</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2010_Jupiter_impact_event" title="2010 Jupiter impact event">2010 Jupiter impact event</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hs-2009-23-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="494" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Hs-2009-23-crop.jpg/220px-Hs-2009-23-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Hs-2009-23-crop.jpg/330px-Hs-2009-23-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Hs-2009-23-crop.jpg/440px-Hs-2009-23-crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hs-2009-23-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a> image taken on July 23, 2009, showing a blemish of about 8,000&#160;km (5,000&#160;mi) long left by the <a href="/wiki/2009_Jupiter_impact_event" title="2009 Jupiter impact event">2009 Jupiter impact</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[155]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Jupiter has been called the Solar System\'s vacuum cleaner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[156]</a></sup> because of its immense <a href="/wiki/Gravity_well" title="Gravity well">gravity well</a> and location near the inner Solar System. It receives the most frequent comet impacts of the Solar System\'s planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[157]</a></sup> It was thought that the planet served to partially shield the inner system from cometary bombardment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_41-6"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-41">[38]</a></sup> However, recent computer simulations suggest that Jupiter does not cause a net decrease in the number of comets that pass through the inner Solar System, as its gravity perturbs their orbits inward roughly as often as it accretes or ejects them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[158]</a></sup> This topic remains controversial among scientists, as some think it draws comets towards Earth from the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> while others think that Jupiter protects Earth from the alleged <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[159]</a></sup> Jupiter experiences about 200 times more <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> impacts than Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_41-7"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-41">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 1997 survey of early astronomical records and drawings suggested that a certain dark surface feature discovered by astronomer <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Cassini</a> in 1690 may have been an impact scar. The survey initially produced eight more candidate sites as potential impact observations that he and others had recorded between 1664 and 1839. It was later determined, however, that these candidate sites had little or no possibility of being the results of the proposed impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[160]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More recent discoveries include the following:\n</p>\n<ol><li>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fireball_(meteor)" title="Fireball (meteor)">fireball</a> was photographed by <i>Voyager 1</i> during its Jupiter encounter in March 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-impact2012_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-impact2012-165">[161]</a></sup></li>\n<li>During the period July 16, 1994, to July 22, 1994, over 20 fragments from the <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> <a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9">Shoemaker&#8211;Levy&#160;9</a> (SL9, formally designated D/1993 F2) collided with Jupiter\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_hemisphere" title="Southern hemisphere">southern hemisphere</a>, providing the first direct observation of a collision between two Solar System objects. This impact provided useful data on the composition of Jupiter\'s atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[163]</a></sup></li>\n<li>On July 19, 2009, an <a href="/wiki/2009_Jupiter_impact_event" title="2009 Jupiter impact event">impact site</a> was discovered at approximately 216 degrees longitude in System 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[165]</a></sup> This impact left behind a black spot in Jupiter\'s atmosphere, similar in size to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oval_BA" title="Oval BA">Oval BA</a>. Infrared observation showed a bright spot where the impact took place, meaning the impact warmed up the lower atmosphere in the area near Jupiter\'s south pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[166]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/2010_Jupiter_impact_event" title="2010 Jupiter impact event">A fireball</a>, smaller than the previous observed impacts, was detected on June 3, 2010, by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Wesley" title="Anthony Wesley">Anthony Wesley</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Amateur_astronomy" title="Amateur astronomy">amateur astronomer</a> in Australia, and was later discovered to have been captured on video by another amateur astronomer in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[167]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Yet another fireball was seen on August 20, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[168]</a></sup></li>\n<li>On September 10, 2012, another fireball was detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-impact2012_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-impact2012-165">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[169]</a></sup></li>\n<li>On March 17, 2016 an asteroid or comet struck and was filmed on video.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[170]</a></sup></li></ol>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="28" 11915</a> &#8211; A <a href="/wiki/Solar_analog" title="Solar analog">solar analog</a> approximately 186 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> from Earth, whose planetary href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">Space exploration</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-caption-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-caption_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This image was taken by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>, using the <a href="/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3" title="Wide Field Camera 3">Wide Field Camera 3</a>, on April 21, 2014. Jupiter\'s atmosphere and its appearance <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter#Dynamics" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">constantly changes</a>, and hence its current appearance today may not resemble what it was when this image was taken. Depicted in this image, however, are a few features that remain consistent, such as the famous <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a>, featured prominently in the lower right of the image, and the planet\'s recognizable banded appearance.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-1bar-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_8-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_8-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_8-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_8-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_8-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Refers to the level of 1 bar atmospheric pressure</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-1bar_b-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1bar_b_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Based on the volume within the level of 1 bar atmospheric pressure</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See for example: <cite class="citation news"><a class="external text" href="http://cbat.eps.harvard.edu/iauc/02800/02844.html" rel="nofollow">"IAUC 2844: Jupiter; 1975h"</a>.
